deprecate_posargs(deprecation_warning, remappable_names, /):\n101:     \"\"\"\n102:     Function/method decorator to deprecate some or all positional arguments.\n103: \n104:     The decorated function {"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_016NuZF6rUZceoD6yK4NwuHx","type":"tool_result","content":"{\"content\":\"1: ==========\\n2: Middleware\\n3: ==========\\n4: \\n5: Middleware is a framework of hooks into Django's request/response processing.\\n6: It's a light, low-level \\\"plugin\\\" system for globally altering Django's input\\n7: or output.\\n8: \\n9: Each middleware component is responsible for doing some specific function. For\\n10: example, Django includes a middleware component,\\n11: :class:`~django.contrib.auth.middleware.AuthenticationMiddleware`, that\\n12: associates users with requests using sessions.\\n13: \\n14: This document explains how middleware works, how you activate middleware, and\\n15: how to write your own middleware. Django ships with some built-in middleware\\n16: you can use right out of the box. They're documented in the :doc:`built-in\\n17: middleware reference </ref/middleware>`.\\n18: \\n19: Writing your own middleware\\n20: ===========================\\n21: \\n22: A middleware factory is a callable that takes a ``get_response`` callable and\\n23: returns a middleware. A middleware is a callable that takes a request and\\n24: returns a response, just like a view.\\n25: \\n26: A middleware can be written as a function that looks like this::\\n27: \\n28:     def simple_middleware(get_response):\\n29:         # One-time configuration and initialization.\\n30: \\n31:         def middleware(request):\\n32:             # Code to be executed for each request before\\n33:             # the view (and later middleware) are called.\\n34: \\n35:             response = get_response(request)\\n36: \\n37:             # Code to be executed for each request/response after\\n38:             # the view is called.\\n39: \\n40:             return response\\n41: \\n42:         return middleware\\n43: \\n44: Or it can be written as a class whose instances are callable, like this::\\n45: \\n46:     class SimpleMiddleware:\\n47:         def __init__(self, get_response):\\n48:             self.get_response = get_response\\n49:             # One-time configuration and initialization.\\n50: \\n51:         def __call__(self, request):\\n52:             # Code to be executed for each request before\\n53:             # the view (and later middleware) are called.\\n54: \\n55:             response = self.get_response(request)\\n56: \\n57:             # Code to be executed for each request/response after\\n58:             # the view is called.\\n59: \\n60:             return response\\n61: \\n62: The ``get_response`` callable provided by Django might be the actual view (if\\n63: this is the last listed middleware) or it might be the next middleware in the\\n64: chain. The current middleware doesn't need to know or care what exactly it is,\\n65: just that it represents whatever comes next.\\n66: \\n67: The above is a slight simplification -- the ``get_response`` callable for the\\n68: last middleware in the chain won't be the actual view but rather a wrapper\\n69: method from the handler which takes care of applying :ref:`view middleware\\n70: <view-middleware>`, calling the view with appropriate URL arguments, and\\n71: applying :ref:`template-response <template-response-middleware>` and\\n72: :ref:`exception <exception-middleware>` middleware.\\n73: \\n74: Middleware can either support only synchronous Python (the default), only\\n75: asynchronous Python, or both. See :ref:`async-middleware` for details of how to\\n76: advertise what you support, and know what kind of request you are getting.\\n77: \\n78: Middleware can live anywhere on your Python path.\\n79: \\n80: ``__init__(get_response)``\\n81: --------------------------\\n82: \\n83: Middleware factories must accept a ``get_response`` argument. You can also\\n84: initialize some global state for the middleware. Keep in mind a couple of\\n85: caveats:\\n86: \\n87: * Django initializes your middleware with only the ``get_response`` argument,\\n88:   so you can't define ``__init__()`` as requiring any other arguments.\\n89: \\n90: * Unlike the ``__call__()`` method which is called once per request,\\n91:   ``__init__()`` is called only *once*, when the web server starts.\\n92: \\n93: Marking middleware as unused\\n94: ----------------------------\\n95: \\n96: It's sometimes useful to determine at startup time whether a piece of\\n97: middleware should be used. In these cases, your middleware's ``__init__()``\\n98: method may raise :exc:`~django.core.exceptions.MiddlewareNotUsed`. Django will\\n99: then remove that middleware from the middleware process and log a debug message\\n100: to the :ref:`django-request-logger` logger when :setting:`DEBUG` is ``True``.\\n101: \\n102: Activating middleware\\n103: =====================\\n104: \\n105: To activate a middleware component, add it to the :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` list in\\n106: your Django settings.\\n107: \\n108: In :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, each middleware component is represented by a string:\\n109: the full Python path to the middleware factory's class or function name. For\\n110: example, here's the default value created by :djadmin:`django-admin\\n111: startproject <startproject>`::\\n112: \\n113:     MIDDLEWARE = [\\n114:         \\\"django.middleware.security.SecurityMiddleware\\\",\\n115:         \\\"django.contrib.sessions.middleware.SessionMiddleware\\\",\\n116:         \\\"django.middleware.common.CommonMiddleware\\\",\\n117:         \\\"django.middleware.csrf.CsrfViewMiddleware\\\",\\n118:         \\\"django.contrib.auth.middleware.AuthenticationMiddleware\\\",\\n119:         \\\"django.contrib.messages.middleware.MessageMiddleware\\\",\\n120:         \\\"django.middleware.clickjacking.XFrameOptionsMiddleware\\\",\\n121:     ]\\n122: \\n123: A Django installation doesn't require any middleware — :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`\\n124: can be empty, if you'd like — but it's strongly suggested that you at least use\\n125: :class:`~django.middleware.common.CommonMiddleware`.\\n126: \\n127: The order in :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` matters because a middleware can depend on\\n128: other middleware. For instance,\\n129: :class:`~django.contrib.auth.middleware.AuthenticationMiddleware` stores the\\n130: authenticated user in the session; therefore, it must run after\\n131: :class:`~django.contrib.sessions.middleware.SessionMiddleware`. See\\n132: :ref:`middleware-ordering` for some common hints about ordering of Django\\n133: middleware classes.\\n134: \\n135: Middleware order and layering\\n136: =============================\\n137: \\n138: During the request phase, before calling the view, Django applies middleware in\\n139: the order it's defined in :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, top-down.\\n140: \\n141: You can think of it like an onion: each middleware class is a \\\"layer\\\" that\\n142: wraps the view, which is in the core of the onion. If the request passes\\n143: through all the layers of the onion (each one calls ``get_response`` to pass\\n144: the request in to the next layer), all the way to the view at the core, the\\n145: response will then pass through every layer (in reverse order) on the way back\\n146: out.\\n147: \\n148: If one of the layers decides to short-circuit and return a response without\\n149: ever calling its ``get_response``, none of the layers of the onion inside that\\n150: layer (including the view) will see the request or the response. The response\\n151: will only return through the same layers that the request passed in through.\\n152: \\n153: Other middleware hooks\\n154: ======================\\n155: \\n156: Besides the basic request/response middleware pattern described earlier, you\\n157: can add three other special methods to class-based middleware:\\n158: \\n159: .. _view-middleware:\\n160: \\n161: ``process_view()``\\n162: ------------------\\n163: \\n164: .. method:: process_view(request, view_func, view_args, view_kwargs)\\n165: \\n166: ``request`` is an :class:`~django.http.HttpRequest` object. ``view_func`` is\\n167: the Python function that Django is about to use. (It's the actual function\\n168: object, not the name of the function as a string.) ``view_args`` is a list of\\n169: positional arguments that will be passed to the view, and ``view_kwargs`` is a\\n170: dictionary of keyword arguments that will be passed to the view. Neither\\n171: ``view_args`` nor ``view_kwargs`` include the first view argument\\n172: (``request``).\\n173: \\n174: ``process_view()`` is called just before Django calls the view.\\n175: \\n176: It should return either ``None`` or an :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse`\\n177: object. If it returns ``None``, Django will continue processing this request,\\n178: executing any other ``process_view()`` middleware and, then, the appropriate\\n179: view. If it returns an :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` object, Django won't\\n180: bother calling the appropriate view; it'll apply response middleware to that\\n181: :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` and return the result.\\n182: \\n183: .. note::\\n184: \\n185:     Accessing :attr:`request.POST <django.http.HttpRequest.POST>` inside\\n186:     middleware before the view runs or in ``process_view()`` will prevent any\\n187:     view running after the middleware from being able to :ref:`modify the\\n188:     upload handlers for the request <modifying_upload_handlers_on_the_fly>`,\\n189:     and should normally be avoided.\\n190: \\n191:     The :class:`~django.middleware.csrf.CsrfViewMiddleware` class can be\\n192:     considered an exception, as it provides the\\n193:     :func:`~django.views.decorators.csrf.csrf_exempt` and\\n194:     :func:`~django.views.decorators.csrf.csrf_protect` decorators which allow\\n195:     views to explicitly control at what point the CSRF validation should occur.\\n196: \\n197: .. _exception-middleware:\\n198: \\n199: ``process_exception()``\\n200: -----------------------\\n201: \\n202: .. method:: process_exception(request, exception)\\n203: \\n204: ``request`` is an :class:`~django.http.HttpRequest` object. ``exception`` is an\\n205: ``Exception`` object raised by the view function.\\n206: \\n207: Django calls ``process_exception()`` when a view raises an exception.\\n208: ``process_exception()`` should return either ``None`` or an\\n209: :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` object. If it returns an\\n210: :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` object, the template response and response\\n211: middleware will be applied and the resulting response returned to the\\n212: browser. Otherwise, :ref:`default exception handling <error-views>` kicks in.\\n213: \\n214: Again, middleware are run in reverse order during the response phase, which\\n215: includes ``process_exception``. If an exception middleware returns a response,\\n216: the ``process_exception`` methods of the middleware classes above that\\n217: middleware won't be called at all.\\n218: \\n219: .. _template-response-middleware:\\n220: \\n221: ``process_template_response()``\\n222: -------------------------------\\n223: \\n224: .. method:: process_template_response(request, response)\\n225: \\n226: ``request`` is an :class:`~django.http.HttpRequest` object. ``response`` is\\n227: the :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse` object (or equivalent)\\n228: returned by a Django view or by a middleware.\\n229: \\n230: ``process_template_response()`` is called just after the view has finished\\n231: executing, if the response instance has a ``render()`` method, indicating that\\n232: it is a :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse` or equivalent.\\n233: \\n234: It must return a response object that implements a ``render`` method. It could\\n235: alter the given ``response`` by changing ``response.template_name`` and\\n236: ``response.context_data``, or it could create and return a brand-new\\n237: :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse` or equivalent.\\n238: \\n239: You don't need to explicitly render responses -- responses will be\\n240: automatically rendered once all template response middleware has been\\n241: called.\\n242: \\n243: Middleware are run in reverse order during the response phase, which\\n244: includes ``process_template_response()``.\\n245: \\n246: Dealing with streaming responses\\n247: ================================\\n248: \\n249: Unlike :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse`,\\n250: :class:`~django.http.StreamingHttpResponse` does not have a ``content``\\n251: attribute. As a result, middleware can no longer assume that all responses\\n252: will have a ``content`` attribute. If they need access to the content, they\\n253: must test for streaming responses and adjust their behavior accordingly::\\n254: \\n255:     if response.streaming:\\n256:         response.streaming_content = wrap_streaming_content(response.streaming_content)\\n257:     else:\\n258:         response.content = alter_content(response.content)\\n259: \\n260: .. note::\\n261: \\n262:     ``streaming_content`` should be assumed to be too large to hold in memory.\\n263:     Response middleware may wrap it in a new generator, but must not consume\\n264:     it. Wrapping is typically implemented as follows::\\n265: \\n266:         def wrap_streaming_content(content):\\n267:             for chunk in content:\\n268:                 yield alter_content(chunk)\\n269: \\n270: :class:`~django.http.StreamingHttpResponse` allows both synchronous and\\n271: asynchronous iterators. The wrapping function must match. Check\\n272: :attr:`StreamingHttpResponse.is_async\\n273: <django.http.StreamingHttpResponse.is_async>` if your middleware needs to\\n274: support both types of iterator.\\n275: \\n276: Exception handling\\n277: ==================\\n278: \\n279: Django automatically converts exceptions raised by the view or by middleware\\n280: into an appropriate HTTP response with an error status code. :ref:`Certain\\n281: exceptions <error-views>` are converted to 4xx status codes, while an unknown\\n282: exception is converted to a 500 status code.\\n283: \\n284: This conversion takes place before and after each middleware (you can think of\\n285: it as the thin film in between each layer of the onion), so that every\\n286: middleware can always rely on getting some kind of HTTP response back from\\n287: calling its ``get_response`` callable. Middleware don't need to worry about\\n288: wrapping their call to ``get_response`` in a ``try/except`` and handling an\\n289: exception that might have been raised by a later middleware or the view. Even\\n290: if the very next middleware in the chain raises an\\n291: :class:`~django.http.Http404` exception, for example, your middleware won't see\\n292: that exception; instead it will get an :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse`\\n293: object with a :attr:`~django.http.HttpResponse.status_code` of 404.\\n294: \\n295: You can set :setting:`DEBUG_PROPAGATE_EXCEPTIONS` to ``True`` to skip this\\n296: conversion and propagate exceptions upward.\\n297: \\n298: .. _async-middleware:\\n299: \\n300: Asynchronous support\\n301: ====================\\n302: \\n303: Middleware can support any combination of synchronous and asynchronous\\n304: requests. Django will adapt requests to fit the middleware's requirements if it\\n305: cannot support both, but at a performance penalty.\\n306: \\n307: By default, Django assumes that your middleware is capable of handling only\\n308: synchronous requests. To change these assumptions, set the following attributes\\n309: on your middleware factory function or class:\\n310: \\n311: * ``sync_capable`` is a boolean indicating if the middleware can handle\\n312:   synchronous requests. Defaults to ``True``.\\n313: \\n314: * ``async_capable`` is a boolean indicating if the middleware can handle\\n315:   asynchronous requests. Defaults to ``False``.\\n316: \\n317: If your middleware has both ``sync_capable = True`` and\\n318: ``async_capable = True``, then Django will pass it the request without\\n319: converting it. In this case, you can work out if your middleware will receive\\n320: async requests by checking if the ``get_response`` object you are passed is a\\n321: coroutine function, using ``inspect.iscoroutinefunction``.\\n322: \\n323: The ``django.utils.decorators`` module contains\\n324: :func:`~django.utils.decorators.sync_only_middleware`,\\n325: :func:`~django.utils.decorators.async_only_middleware`, and\\n326: :func:`~django.utils.decorators.sync_and_async_middleware` decorators that\\n327: allow you to apply these flags to middleware factory functions.\\n328: \\n329: The returned callable must match the sync or async nature of the\\n330: ``get_response`` method. If you have an asynchronous ``get_response``, you must\\n331: return a coroutine function (``async def``).\\n332: \\n333: ``process_view``, ``process_template_response`` and ``process_exception``\\n334: methods, if they are provided, should also be adapted to match the sync/async\\n335: mode. However, Django will individually adapt them as required if you do not,\\n336: at an additional performance penalty.\\n337: \\n338: Here's an example of how to create a middleware function that supports both::\\n339: \\n340:     from inspect import iscoroutinefunction\\n341:     from django.utils.decorators import sync_and_async_middleware\\n342: \\n343: \\n344:     @sync_and_async_middleware\\n345:     def simple_middleware(get_response):\\n346:         # One-time configuration and initialization goes here.\\n347:         if iscoroutinefunction(get_response):\\n348: \\n349:             async def middleware(request):\\n350:                 # Do something here!\\n351:                 response = await get_response(request)\\n352:                 return response\\n353: \\n354:         else:\\n355: \\n356:             def middleware(request):\\n357:                 # Do something here!\\n358:                 response = get_response(request)\\n359:                 return response\\n360: \\n361:         return middleware\\n362: \\n363: .. note::\\n364: \\n365:     If you declare a hybrid middleware that supports both synchronous and\\n366:     asynchronous calls, the kind of call you get may not match the underlying\\n367:     view. Django will optimize the middleware call stack to have as few\\n368:     sync/async transitions as possible.\\n369: \\n370:     Thus, even if you are wrapping an async view, you may be called in sync\\n371:     mode if there is other, synchronous middleware between you and the view.\\n372: \\n373: When using an asynchronous class-based middleware, you must ensure that\\n374: instances are correctly marked as coroutine functions::\\n375: \\n376:     from inspect import iscoroutinefunction, markcoroutinefunction\\n377: \\n378: \\n379:     class AsyncMiddleware:\\n380:         async_capable = True\\n381:         sync_capable = False\\n382: \\n383:         def __init__(self, get_response):\\n384:             self.get_response = get_response\\n385:             if iscoroutinefunction(self.get_response):\\n386:                 markcoroutinefunction(self)\\n387: \\n388:         async def __call__(self, request):\\n389:             response = await self.get_response(request)\\n390:             # Some logic ...\\n391:             return response\\n392: \\n393: .. _upgrading-middleware:\\n394: \\n395: Upgrading pre-Django 1.10-style middleware\\n396: ==========================================\\n397: \\n398: .. class:: django.utils.deprecation.MiddlewareMixin\\n399:     :module:\\n400: \\n401: Django provides ``django.utils.deprecation.MiddlewareMixin`` to ease creating\\n402: middleware classes that are compatible with both :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` and the\\n403: old ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, and support synchronous and asynchronous requests.\\n404: All middleware classes included with Django are compatible with both settings.\\n405: \\n406: The mixin provides an ``__init__()`` method that requires a ``get_response``\\n407: argument and stores it in ``self.get_response``.\\n408: \\n409: The ``__call__()`` method:\\n410: \\n411: #. Calls ``self.process_request(request)`` (if defined).\\n412: #. Calls ``self.get_response(request)`` to get the response from later\\n413:    middleware and the view.\\n414: #. Calls ``self.process_response(request, response)`` (if defined).\\n415: #. Returns the response.\\n416: \\n417: If used with ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, the ``__call__()`` method will\\n418: never be used; Django calls ``process_request()`` and ``process_response()``\\n419: directly.\\n420: \\n421: In most cases, inheriting from this mixin will be sufficient to make an\\n422: old-style middleware compatible with the new system with sufficient\\n423: backwards-compatibility. The new short-circuiting semantics will be harmless or\\n424: even beneficial to the existing middleware. In a few cases, a middleware class\\n425: may need some changes to adjust to the new semantics.\\n426: \\n427: These are the behavioral differences between using :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` and\\n428: ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``:\\n429: \\n430: #. Under ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, every middleware will always have its\\n431:    ``process_response`` method called, even if an earlier middleware\\n432:    short-circuited by returning a response from its ``process_request``\\n433:    method. Under :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, middleware behaves more like an onion:\\n434:    the layers that a response goes through on the way out are the same layers\\n435:    that saw the request on the way in. If a middleware short-circuits, only\\n436:    that middleware and the ones before it in :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` will see the\\n437:    response.\\n438: \\n439: #. Under ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, ``process_exception`` is applied to\\n440:    exceptions raised from a middleware ``process_request`` method. Under\\n441:    :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, ``process_exception`` applies only to exceptions\\n442:    raised from the view (or from the ``render`` method of a\\n443:    :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse`). Exceptions raised from\\n444:    a middleware are converted to the appropriate HTTP response and then passed\\n445:    to the next middleware.\\n446: \\n447: #. Under ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, if a ``process_response`` method raises\\n448:    an exception, the ``process_response`` methods of all earlier middleware are\\n449:    skipped and a ``500 Internal Server Error`` HTTP response is always\\n450:    returned (even if the exception raised was e.g. an\\n451:    :class:`~django.http.Http404`). Under :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, an exception\\n452:    raised from a middleware will immediately be converted to the appropriate\\n453:    HTTP response, and then the next middleware in line will see that\\n454:    response. Middleware are never skipped due to a middleware raising an\\n455:    exception.\\n456: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/django--674eda1c@674eda1c03a3187905f48afee0f15226aa62fdf3/-/blob/docs/topics/http/middleware.txt\"}"}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"81a8b0db-1e09-4b2a-99ee-598a1e3c7cf3","uuid":"8320ec6e-15d7-40b5-aa0d-5ee3a9e934cd","tool_use_result":{"content":"{\"content\":\"1: ==========\\n2: Middleware\\n3: ==========\\n4: \\n5: Middleware is a framework of hooks into Django's request/response processing.\\n6: It's a light, low-level \\\"plugin\\\" system for globally altering Django's input\\n7: or output.\\n8: \\n9: Each middleware component is responsible for doing some specific function. For\\n10: example, Django includes a middleware component,\\n11: :class:`~django.contrib.auth.middleware.AuthenticationMiddleware`, that\\n12: associates users with requests using sessions.\\n13: \\n14: This document explains how middleware works, how you activate middleware, and\\n15: how to write your own middleware. Django ships with some built-in middleware\\n16: you can use right out of the box. They're documented in the :doc:`built-in\\n17: middleware reference </ref/middleware>`.\\n18: \\n19: Writing your own middleware\\n20: ===========================\\n21: \\n22: A middleware factory is a callable that takes a ``get_response`` callable and\\n23: returns a middleware. A middleware is a callable that takes a request and\\n24: returns a response, just like a view.\\n25: \\n26: A middleware can be written as a function that looks like this::\\n27: \\n28:     def simple_middleware(get_response):\\n29:         # One-time configuration and initialization.\\n30: \\n31:         def middleware(request):\\n32:             # Code to be executed for each request before\\n33:             # the view (and later middleware) are called.\\n34: \\n35:             response = get_response(request)\\n36: \\n37:             # Code to be executed for each request/response after\\n38:             # the view is called.\\n39: \\n40:             return response\\n41: \\n42:         return middleware\\n43: \\n44: Or it can be written as a class whose instances are callable, like this::\\n45: \\n46:     class SimpleMiddleware:\\n47:         def __init__(self, get_response):\\n48:             self.get_response = get_response\\n49:             # One-time configuration and initialization.\\n50: \\n51:         def __call__(self, request):\\n52:             # Code to be executed for each request before\\n53:             # the view (and later middleware) are called.\\n54: \\n55:             response = self.get_response(request)\\n56: \\n57:             # Code to be executed for each request/response after\\n58:             # the view is called.\\n59: \\n60:             return response\\n61: \\n62: The ``get_response`` callable provided by Django might be the actual view (if\\n63: this is the last listed middleware) or it might be the next middleware in the\\n64: chain. The current middleware doesn't need to know or care what exactly it is,\\n65: just that it represents whatever comes next.\\n66: \\n67: The above is a slight simplification -- the ``get_response`` callable for the\\n68: last middleware in the chain won't be the actual view but rather a wrapper\\n69: method from the handler which takes care of applying :ref:`view middleware\\n70: <view-middleware>`, calling the view with appropriate URL arguments, and\\n71: applying :ref:`template-response <template-response-middleware>` and\\n72: :ref:`exception <exception-middleware>` middleware.\\n73: \\n74: Middleware can either support only synchronous Python (the default), only\\n75: asynchronous Python, or both. See :ref:`async-middleware` for details of how to\\n76: advertise what you support, and know what kind of request you are getting.\\n77: \\n78: Middleware can live anywhere on your Python path.\\n79: \\n80: ``__init__(get_response)``\\n81: --------------------------\\n82: \\n83: Middleware factories must accept a ``get_response`` argument. You can also\\n84: initialize some global state for the middleware. Keep in mind a couple of\\n85: caveats:\\n86: \\n87: * Django initializes your middleware with only the ``get_response`` argument,\\n88:   so you can't define ``__init__()`` as requiring any other arguments.\\n89: \\n90: * Unlike the ``__call__()`` method which is called once per request,\\n91:   ``__init__()`` is called only *once*, when the web server starts.\\n92: \\n93: Marking middleware as unused\\n94: ----------------------------\\n95: \\n96: It's sometimes useful to determine at startup time whether a piece of\\n97: middleware should be used. In these cases, your middleware's ``__init__()``\\n98: method may raise :exc:`~django.core.exceptions.MiddlewareNotUsed`. Django will\\n99: then remove that middleware from the middleware process and log a debug message\\n100: to the :ref:`django-request-logger` logger when :setting:`DEBUG` is ``True``.\\n101: \\n102: Activating middleware\\n103: =====================\\n104: \\n105: To activate a middleware component, add it to the :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` list in\\n106: your Django settings.\\n107: \\n108: In :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, each middleware component is represented by a string:\\n109: the full Python path to the middleware factory's class or function name. For\\n110: example, here's the default value created by :djadmin:`django-admin\\n111: startproject <startproject>`::\\n112: \\n113:     MIDDLEWARE = [\\n114:         \\\"django.middleware.security.SecurityMiddleware\\\",\\n115:         \\\"django.contrib.sessions.middleware.SessionMiddleware\\\",\\n116:         \\\"django.middleware.common.CommonMiddleware\\\",\\n117:         \\\"django.middleware.csrf.CsrfViewMiddleware\\\",\\n118:         \\\"django.contrib.auth.middleware.AuthenticationMiddleware\\\",\\n119:         \\\"django.contrib.messages.middleware.MessageMiddleware\\\",\\n120:         \\\"django.middleware.clickjacking.XFrameOptionsMiddleware\\\",\\n121:     ]\\n122: \\n123: A Django installation doesn't require any middleware — :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`\\n124: can be empty, if you'd like — but it's strongly suggested that you at least use\\n125: :class:`~django.middleware.common.CommonMiddleware`.\\n126: \\n127: The order in :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` matters because a middleware can depend on\\n128: other middleware. For instance,\\n129: :class:`~django.contrib.auth.middleware.AuthenticationMiddleware` stores the\\n130: authenticated user in the session; therefore, it must run after\\n131: :class:`~django.contrib.sessions.middleware.SessionMiddleware`. See\\n132: :ref:`middleware-ordering` for some common hints about ordering of Django\\n133: middleware classes.\\n134: \\n135: Middleware order and layering\\n136: =============================\\n137: \\n138: During the request phase, before calling the view, Django applies middleware in\\n139: the order it's defined in :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, top-down.\\n140: \\n141: You can think of it like an onion: each middleware class is a \\\"layer\\\" that\\n142: wraps the view, which is in the core of the onion. If the request passes\\n143: through all the layers of the onion (each one calls ``get_response`` to pass\\n144: the request in to the next layer), all the way to the view at the core, the\\n145: response will then pass through every layer (in reverse order) on the way back\\n146: out.\\n147: \\n148: If one of the layers decides to short-circuit and return a response without\\n149: ever calling its ``get_response``, none of the layers of the onion inside that\\n150: layer (including the view) will see the request or the response. The response\\n151: will only return through the same layers that the request passed in through.\\n152: \\n153: Other middleware hooks\\n154: ======================\\n155: \\n156: Besides the basic request/response middleware pattern described earlier, you\\n157: can add three other special methods to class-based middleware:\\n158: \\n159: .. _view-middleware:\\n160: \\n161: ``process_view()``\\n162: ------------------\\n163: \\n164: .. method:: process_view(request, view_func, view_args, view_kwargs)\\n165: \\n166: ``request`` is an :class:`~django.http.HttpRequest` object. ``view_func`` is\\n167: the Python function that Django is about to use. (It's the actual function\\n168: object, not the name of the function as a string.) ``view_args`` is a list of\\n169: positional arguments that will be passed to the view, and ``view_kwargs`` is a\\n170: dictionary of keyword arguments that will be passed to the view. Neither\\n171: ``view_args`` nor ``view_kwargs`` include the first view argument\\n172: (``request``).\\n173: \\n174: ``process_view()`` is called just before Django calls the view.\\n175: \\n176: It should return either ``None`` or an :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse`\\n177: object. If it returns ``None``, Django will continue processing this request,\\n178: executing any other ``process_view()`` middleware and, then, the appropriate\\n179: view. If it returns an :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` object, Django won't\\n180: bother calling the appropriate view; it'll apply response middleware to that\\n181: :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` and return the result.\\n182: \\n183: .. note::\\n184: \\n185:     Accessing :attr:`request.POST <django.http.HttpRequest.POST>` inside\\n186:     middleware before the view runs or in ``process_view()`` will prevent any\\n187:     view running after the middleware from being able to :ref:`modify the\\n188:     upload handlers for the request <modifying_upload_handlers_on_the_fly>`,\\n189:     and should normally be avoided.\\n190: \\n191:     The :class:`~django.middleware.csrf.CsrfViewMiddleware` class can be\\n192:     considered an exception, as it provides the\\n193:     :func:`~django.views.decorators.csrf.csrf_exempt` and\\n194:     :func:`~django.views.decorators.csrf.csrf_protect` decorators which allow\\n195:     views to explicitly control at what point the CSRF validation should occur.\\n196: \\n197: .. _exception-middleware:\\n198: \\n199: ``process_exception()``\\n200: -----------------------\\n201: \\n202: .. method:: process_exception(request, exception)\\n203: \\n204: ``request`` is an :class:`~django.http.HttpRequest` object. ``exception`` is an\\n205: ``Exception`` object raised by the view function.\\n206: \\n207: Django calls ``process_exception()`` when a view raises an exception.\\n208: ``process_exception()`` should return either ``None`` or an\\n209: :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` object. If it returns an\\n210: :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` object, the template response and response\\n211: middleware will be applied and the resulting response returned to the\\n212: browser. Otherwise, :ref:`default exception handling <error-views>` kicks in.\\n213: \\n214: Again, middleware are run in reverse order during the response phase, which\\n215: includes ``process_exception``. If an exception middleware returns a response,\\n216: the ``process_exception`` methods of the middleware classes above that\\n217: middleware won't be called at all.\\n218: \\n219: .. _template-response-middleware:\\n220: \\n221: ``process_template_response()``\\n222: -------------------------------\\n223: \\n224: .. method:: process_template_response(request, response)\\n225: \\n226: ``request`` is an :class:`~django.http.HttpRequest` object. ``response`` is\\n227: the :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse` object (or equivalent)\\n228: returned by a Django view or by a middleware.\\n229: \\n230: ``process_template_response()`` is called just after the view has finished\\n231: executing, if the response instance has a ``render()`` method, indicating that\\n232: it is a :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse` or equivalent.\\n233: \\n234: It must return a response object that implements a ``render`` method. It could\\n235: alter the given ``response`` by changing ``response.template_name`` and\\n236: ``response.context_data``, or it could create and return a brand-new\\n237: :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse` or equivalent.\\n238: \\n239: You don't need to explicitly render responses -- responses will be\\n240: automatically rendered once all template response middleware has been\\n241: called.\\n242: \\n243: Middleware are run in reverse order during the response phase, which\\n244: includes ``process_template_response()``.\\n245: \\n246: Dealing with streaming responses\\n247: ================================\\n248: \\n249: Unlike :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse`,\\n250: :class:`~django.http.StreamingHttpResponse` does not have a ``content``\\n251: attribute. As a result, middleware can no longer assume that all responses\\n252: will have a ``content`` attribute. If they need access to the content, they\\n253: must test for streaming responses and adjust their behavior accordingly::\\n254: \\n255:     if response.streaming:\\n256:         response.streaming_content = wrap_streaming_content(response.streaming_content)\\n257:     else:\\n258:         response.content = alter_content(response.content)\\n259: \\n260: .. note::\\n261: \\n262:     ``streaming_content`` should be assumed to be too large to hold in memory.\\n263:     Response middleware may wrap it in a new generator, but must not consume\\n264:     it. Wrapping is typically implemented as follows::\\n265: \\n266:         def wrap_streaming_content(content):\\n267:             for chunk in content:\\n268:                 yield alter_content(chunk)\\n269: \\n270: :class:`~django.http.StreamingHttpResponse` allows both synchronous and\\n271: asynchronous iterators. The wrapping function must match. Check\\n272: :attr:`StreamingHttpResponse.is_async\\n273: <django.http.StreamingHttpResponse.is_async>` if your middleware needs to\\n274: support both types of iterator.\\n275: \\n276: Exception handling\\n277: ==================\\n278: \\n279: Django automatically converts exceptions raised by the view or by middleware\\n280: into an appropriate HTTP response with an error status code. :ref:`Certain\\n281: exceptions <error-views>` are converted to 4xx status codes, while an unknown\\n282: exception is converted to a 500 status code.\\n283: \\n284: This conversion takes place before and after each middleware (you can think of\\n285: it as the thin film in between each layer of the onion), so that every\\n286: middleware can always rely on getting some kind of HTTP response back from\\n287: calling its ``get_response`` callable. Middleware don't need to worry about\\n288: wrapping their call to ``get_response`` in a ``try/except`` and handling an\\n289: exception that might have been raised by a later middleware or the view. Even\\n290: if the very next middleware in the chain raises an\\n291: :class:`~django.http.Http404` exception, for example, your middleware won't see\\n292: that exception; instead it will get an :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse`\\n293: object with a :attr:`~django.http.HttpResponse.status_code` of 404.\\n294: \\n295: You can set :setting:`DEBUG_PROPAGATE_EXCEPTIONS` to ``True`` to skip this\\n296: conversion and propagate exceptions upward.\\n297: \\n298: .. _async-middleware:\\n299: \\n300: Asynchronous support\\n301: ====================\\n302: \\n303: Middleware can support any combination of synchronous and asynchronous\\n304: requests. Django will adapt requests to fit the middleware's requirements if it\\n305: cannot support both, but at a performance penalty.\\n306: \\n307: By default, Django assumes that your middleware is capable of handling only\\n308: synchronous requests. To change these assumptions, set the following attributes\\n309: on your middleware factory function or class:\\n310: \\n311: * ``sync_capable`` is a boolean indicating if the middleware can handle\\n312:   synchronous requests. Defaults to ``True``.\\n313: \\n314: * ``async_capable`` is a boolean indicating if the middleware can handle\\n315:   asynchronous requests. Defaults to ``False``.\\n316: \\n317: If your middleware has both ``sync_capable = True`` and\\n318: ``async_capable = True``, then Django will pass it the request without\\n319: converting it. In this case, you can work out if your middleware will receive\\n320: async requests by checking if the ``get_response`` object you are passed is a\\n321: coroutine function, using ``inspect.iscoroutinefunction``.\\n322: \\n323: The ``django.utils.decorators`` module contains\\n324: :func:`~django.utils.decorators.sync_only_middleware`,\\n325: :func:`~django.utils.decorators.async_only_middleware`, and\\n326: :func:`~django.utils.decorators.sync_and_async_middleware` decorators that\\n327: allow you to apply these flags to middleware factory functions.\\n328: \\n329: The returned callable must match the sync or async nature of the\\n330: ``get_response`` method. If you have an asynchronous ``get_response``, you must\\n331: return a coroutine function (``async def``).\\n332: \\n333: ``process_view``, ``process_template_response`` and ``process_exception``\\n334: methods, if they are provided, should also be adapted to match the sync/async\\n335: mode. However, Django will individually adapt them as required if you do not,\\n336: at an additional performance penalty.\\n337: \\n338: Here's an example of how to create a middleware function that supports both::\\n339: \\n340:     from inspect import iscoroutinefunction\\n341:     from django.utils.decorators import sync_and_async_middleware\\n342: \\n343: \\n344:     @sync_and_async_middleware\\n345:     def simple_middleware(get_response):\\n346:         # One-time configuration and initialization goes here.\\n347:         if iscoroutinefunction(get_response):\\n348: \\n349:             async def middleware(request):\\n350:                 # Do something here!\\n351:                 response = await get_response(request)\\n352:                 return response\\n353: \\n354:         else:\\n355: \\n356:             def middleware(request):\\n357:                 # Do something here!\\n358:                 response = get_response(request)\\n359:                 return response\\n360: \\n361:         return middleware\\n362: \\n363: .. note::\\n364: \\n365:     If you declare a hybrid middleware that supports both synchronous and\\n366:     asynchronous calls, the kind of call you get may not match the underlying\\n367:     view. Django will optimize the middleware call stack to have as few\\n368:     sync/async transitions as possible.\\n369: \\n370:     Thus, even if you are wrapping an async view, you may be called in sync\\n371:     mode if there is other, synchronous middleware between you and the view.\\n372: \\n373: When using an asynchronous class-based middleware, you must ensure that\\n374: instances are correctly marked as coroutine functions::\\n375: \\n376:     from inspect import iscoroutinefunction, markcoroutinefunction\\n377: \\n378: \\n379:     class AsyncMiddleware:\\n380:         async_capable = True\\n381:         sync_capable = False\\n382: \\n383:         def __init__(self, get_response):\\n384:             self.get_response = get_response\\n385:             if iscoroutinefunction(self.get_response):\\n386:                 markcoroutinefunction(self)\\n387: \\n388:         async def __call__(self, request):\\n389:             response = await self.get_response(request)\\n390:             # Some logic ...\\n391:             return response\\n392: \\n393: .. _upgrading-middleware:\\n394: \\n395: Upgrading pre-Django 1.10-style middleware\\n396: ==========================================\\n397: \\n398: .. class:: django.utils.deprecation.MiddlewareMixin\\n399:     :module:\\n400: \\n401: Django provides ``django.utils.deprecation.MiddlewareMixin`` to ease creating\\n402: middleware classes that are compatible with both :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` and the\\n403: old ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, and support synchronous and asynchronous requests.\\n404: All middleware classes included with Django are compatible with both settings.\\n405: \\n406: The mixin provides an ``__init__()`` method that requires a ``get_response``\\n407: argument and stores it in ``self.get_response``.\\n408: \\n409: The ``__call__()`` method:\\n410: \\n411: #. Calls ``self.process_request(request)`` (if defined).\\n412: #. Calls ``self.get_response(request)`` to get the response from later\\n413:    middleware and the view.\\n414: #. Calls ``self.process_response(request, response)`` (if defined).\\n415: #. Returns the response.\\n416: \\n417: If used with ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, the ``__call__()`` method will\\n418: never be used; Django calls ``process_request()`` and ``process_response()``\\n419: directly.\\n420: \\n421: In most cases, inheriting from this mixin will be sufficient to make an\\n422: old-style middleware compatible with the new system with sufficient\\n423: backwards-compatibility. The new short-circuiting semantics will be harmless or\\n424: even beneficial to the existing middleware. In a few cases, a middleware class\\n425: may need some changes to adjust to the new semantics.\\n426: \\n427: These are the behavioral differences between using :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` and\\n428: ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``:\\n429: \\n430: #. Under ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, every middleware will always have its\\n431:    ``process_response`` method called, even if an earlier middleware\\n432:    short-circuited by returning a response from its ``process_request``\\n433:    method. Under :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, middleware behaves more like an onion:\\n434:    the layers that a response goes through on the way out are the same layers\\n435:    that saw the request on the way in. If a middleware short-circuits, only\\n436:    that middleware and the ones before it in :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` will see the\\n437:    response.\\n438: \\n439: #. Under ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, ``process_exception`` is applied to\\n440:    exceptions raised from a middleware ``process_request`` method. Under\\n441:    :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, ``process_exception`` applies only to exceptions\\n442:    raised from the view (or from the ``render`` method of a\\n443:    :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse`). Exceptions raised from\\n444:    a middleware are converted to the appropriate HTTP response and then passed\\n445:    to the next middleware.\\n446: \\n447: #. Under ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, if a ``process_response`` method raises\\n448:    an exception, the ``process_response`` methods of all earlier middleware are\\n449:    skipped and a ``500 Internal Server Error`` HTTP response is always\\n450:    returned (even if the exception raised was e.g. an\\n451:    :class:`~django.http.Http404`). Under :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, an exception\\n452:    raised from a middleware will immediately be converted to the appropriate\\n453:    HTTP response, and then the next middleware in line will see that\\n454:    response. Middleware are never skipped due to a middleware raising an\\n455:    exception.\\n456: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/django--674eda1c@674eda1c03a3187905f48afee0f15226aa62fdf3/-/blob/docs/topics/http/middleware.txt\"}","structuredContent":{"content":"1: ==========\n2: Middleware\n3: ==========\n4: \n5: Middleware is a framework of hooks into Django's request/response processing.\n6: It's a light, low-level \"plugin\" system for globally altering Django's input\n7: or output.\n8: \n9: Each middleware component is responsible for doing some specific function. For\n10: example, Django includes a middleware component,\n11: :class:`~django.contrib.auth.middleware.AuthenticationMiddleware`, that\n12: associates users with requests using sessions.\n13: \n14: This document explains how middleware works, how you activate middleware, and\n15: how to write your own middleware. Django ships with some built-in middleware\n16: you can use right out of the box. They're documented in the :doc:`built-in\n17: middleware reference </ref/middleware>`.\n18: \n19: Writing your own middleware\n20: ===========================\n21: \n22: A middleware factory is a callable that takes a ``get_response`` callable and\n23: returns a middleware. A middleware is a callable that takes a request and\n24: returns a response, just like a view.\n25: \n26: A middleware can be written as a function that looks like this::\n27: \n28:     def simple_middleware(get_response):\n29:         # One-time configuration and initialization.\n30: \n31:         def middleware(request):\n32:             # Code to be executed for each request before\n33:             # the view (and later middleware) are called.\n34: \n35:             response = get_response(request)\n36: \n37:             # Code to be executed for each request/response after\n38:             # the view is called.\n39: \n40:             return response\n41: \n42:         return middleware\n43: \n44: Or it can be written as a class whose instances are callable, like this::\n45: \n46:     class SimpleMiddleware:\n47:         def __init__(self, get_response):\n48:             self.get_response = get_response\n49:             # One-time configuration and initialization.\n50: \n51:         def __call__(self, request):\n52:             # Code to be executed for each request before\n53:             # the view (and later middleware) are called.\n54: \n55:             response = self.get_response(request)\n56: \n57:             # Code to be executed for each request/response after\n58:             # the view is called.\n59: \n60:             return response\n61: \n62: The ``get_response`` callable provided by Django might be the actual view (if\n63: this is the last listed middleware) or it might be the next middleware in the\n64: chain. The current middleware doesn't need to know or care what exactly it is,\n65: just that it represents whatever comes next.\n66: \n67: The above is a slight simplification -- the ``get_response`` callable for the\n68: last middleware in the chain won't be the actual view but rather a wrapper\n69: method from the handler which takes care of applying :ref:`view middleware\n70: <view-middleware>`, calling the view with appropriate URL arguments, and\n71: applying :ref:`template-response <template-response-middleware>` and\n72: :ref:`exception <exception-middleware>` middleware.\n73: \n74: Middleware can either support only synchronous Python (the default), only\n75: asynchronous Python, or both. See :ref:`async-middleware` for details of how to\n76: advertise what you support, and know what kind of request you are getting.\n77: \n78: Middleware can live anywhere on your Python path.\n79: \n80: ``__init__(get_response)``\n81: --------------------------\n82: \n83: Middleware factories must accept a ``get_response`` argument. You can also\n84: initialize some global state for the middleware. Keep in mind a couple of\n85: caveats:\n86: \n87: * Django initializes your middleware with only the ``get_response`` argument,\n88:   so you can't define ``__init__()`` as requiring any other arguments.\n89: \n90: * Unlike the ``__call__()`` method which is called once per request,\n91:   ``__init__()`` is called only *once*, when the web server starts.\n92: \n93: Marking middleware as unused\n94: ----------------------------\n95: \n96: It's sometimes useful to determine at startup time whether a piece of\n97: middleware should be used. In these cases, your middleware's ``__init__()``\n98: method may raise :exc:`~django.core.exceptions.MiddlewareNotUsed`. Django will\n99: then remove that middleware from the middleware process and log a debug message\n100: to the :ref:`django-request-logger` logger when :setting:`DEBUG` is ``True``.\n101: \n102: Activating middleware\n103: =====================\n104: \n105: To activate a middleware component, add it to the :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` list in\n106: your Django settings.\n107: \n108: In :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, each middleware component is represented by a string:\n109: the full Python path to the middleware factory's class or function name. For\n110: example, here's the default value created by :djadmin:`django-admin\n111: startproject <startproject>`::\n112: \n113:     MIDDLEWARE = [\n114:         \"django.middleware.security.SecurityMiddleware\",\n115:         \"django.contrib.sessions.middleware.SessionMiddleware\",\n116:         \"django.middleware.common.CommonMiddleware\",\n117:         \"django.middleware.csrf.CsrfViewMiddleware\",\n118:         \"django.contrib.auth.middleware.AuthenticationMiddleware\",\n119:         \"django.contrib.messages.middleware.MessageMiddleware\",\n120:         \"django.middleware.clickjacking.XFrameOptionsMiddleware\",\n121:     ]\n122: \n123: A Django installation doesn't require any middleware — :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`\n124: can be empty, if you'd like — but it's strongly suggested that you at least use\n125: :class:`~django.middleware.common.CommonMiddleware`.\n126: \n127: The order in :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` matters because a middleware can depend on\n128: other middleware. For instance,\n129: :class:`~django.contrib.auth.middleware.AuthenticationMiddleware` stores the\n130: authenticated user in the session; therefore, it must run after\n131: :class:`~django.contrib.sessions.middleware.SessionMiddleware`. See\n132: :ref:`middleware-ordering` for some common hints about ordering of Django\n133: middleware classes.\n134: \n135: Middleware order and layering\n136: =============================\n137: \n138: During the request phase, before calling the view, Django applies middleware in\n139: the order it's defined in :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, top-down.\n140: \n141: You can think of it like an onion: each middleware class is a \"layer\" that\n142: wraps the view, which is in the core of the onion. If the request passes\n143: through all the layers of the onion (each one calls ``get_response`` to pass\n144: the request in to the next layer), all the way to the view at the core, the\n145: response will then pass through every layer (in reverse order) on the way back\n146: out.\n147: \n148: If one of the layers decides to short-circuit and return a response without\n149: ever calling its ``get_response``, none of the layers of the onion inside that\n150: layer (including the view) will see the request or the response. The response\n151: will only return through the same layers that the request passed in through.\n152: \n153: Other middleware hooks\n154: ======================\n155: \n156: Besides the basic request/response middleware pattern described earlier, you\n157: can add three other special methods to class-based middleware:\n158: \n159: .. _view-middleware:\n160: \n161: ``process_view()``\n162: ------------------\n163: \n164: .. method:: process_view(request, view_func, view_args, view_kwargs)\n165: \n166: ``request`` is an :class:`~django.http.HttpRequest` object. ``view_func`` is\n167: the Python function that Django is about to use. (It's the actual function\n168: object, not the name of the function as a string.) ``view_args`` is a list of\n169: positional arguments that will be passed to the view, and ``view_kwargs`` is a\n170: dictionary of keyword arguments that will be passed to the view. Neither\n171: ``view_args`` nor ``view_kwargs`` include the first view argument\n172: (``request``).\n173: \n174: ``process_view()`` is called just before Django calls the view.\n175: \n176: It should return either ``None`` or an :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse`\n177: object. If it returns ``None``, Django will continue processing this request,\n178: executing any other ``process_view()`` middleware and, then, the appropriate\n179: view. If it returns an :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` object, Django won't\n180: bother calling the appropriate view; it'll apply response middleware to that\n181: :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` and return the result.\n182: \n183: .. note::\n184: \n185:     Accessing :attr:`request.POST <django.http.HttpRequest.POST>` inside\n186:     middleware before the view runs or in ``process_view()`` will prevent any\n187:     view running after the middleware from being able to :ref:`modify the\n188:     upload handlers for the request <modifying_upload_handlers_on_the_fly>`,\n189:     and should normally be avoided.\n190: \n191:     The :class:`~django.middleware.csrf.CsrfViewMiddleware` class can be\n192:     considered an exception, as it provides the\n193:     :func:`~django.views.decorators.csrf.csrf_exempt` and\n194:     :func:`~django.views.decorators.csrf.csrf_protect` decorators which allow\n195:     views to explicitly control at what point the CSRF validation should occur.\n196: \n197: .. _exception-middleware:\n198: \n199: ``process_exception()``\n200: -----------------------\n201: \n202: .. method:: process_exception(request, exception)\n203: \n204: ``request`` is an :class:`~django.http.HttpRequest` object. ``exception`` is an\n205: ``Exception`` object raised by the view function.\n206: \n207: Django calls ``process_exception()`` when a view raises an exception.\n208: ``process_exception()`` should return either ``None`` or an\n209: :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` object. If it returns an\n210: :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` object, the template response and response\n211: middleware will be applied and the resulting response returned to the\n212: browser. Otherwise, :ref:`default exception handling <error-views>` kicks in.\n213: \n214: Again, middleware are run in reverse order during the response phase, which\n215: includes ``process_exception``. If an exception middleware returns a response,\n216: the ``process_exception`` methods of the middleware classes above that\n217: middleware won't be called at all.\n218: \n219: .. _template-response-middleware:\n220: \n221: ``process_template_response()``\n222: -------------------------------\n223: \n224: .. method:: process_template_response(request, response)\n225: \n226: ``request`` is an :class:`~django.http.HttpRequest` object. ``response`` is\n227: the :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse` object (or equivalent)\n228: returned by a Django view or by a middleware.\n229: \n230: ``process_template_response()`` is called just after the view has finished\n231: executing, if the response instance has a ``render()`` method, indicating that\n232: it is a :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse` or equivalent.\n233: \n234: It must return a response object that implements a ``render`` method. It could\n235: alter the given ``response`` by changing ``response.template_name`` and\n236: ``response.context_data``, or it could create and return a brand-new\n237: :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse` or equivalent.\n238: \n239: You don't need to explicitly render responses -- responses will be\n240: automatically rendered once all template response middleware has been\n241: called.\n242: \n243: Middleware are run in reverse order during the response phase, which\n244: includes ``process_template_response()``.\n245: \n246: Dealing with streaming responses\n247: ================================\n248: \n249: Unlike :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse`,\n250: :class:`~django.http.StreamingHttpResponse` does not have a ``content``\n251: attribute. As a result, middleware can no longer assume that all responses\n252: will have a ``content`` attribute. If they need access to the content, they\n253: must test for streaming responses and adjust their behavior accordingly::\n254: \n255:     if response.streaming:\n256:         response.streaming_content = wrap_streaming_content(response.streaming_content)\n257:     else:\n258:         response.content = alter_content(response.content)\n259: \n260: .. note::\n261: \n262:     ``streaming_content`` should be assumed to be too large to hold in memory.\n263:     Response middleware may wrap it in a new generator, but must not consume\n264:     it. Wrapping is typically implemented as follows::\n265: \n266:         def wrap_streaming_content(content):\n267:             for chunk in content:\n268:                 yield alter_content(chunk)\n269: \n270: :class:`~django.http.StreamingHttpResponse` allows both synchronous and\n271: asynchronous iterators. The wrapping function must match. Check\n272: :attr:`StreamingHttpResponse.is_async\n273: <django.http.StreamingHttpResponse.is_async>` if your middleware needs to\n274: support both types of iterator.\n275: \n276: Exception handling\n277: ==================\n278: \n279: Django automatically converts exceptions raised by the view or by middleware\n280: into an appropriate HTTP response with an error status code. :ref:`Certain\n281: exceptions <error-views>` are converted to 4xx status codes, while an unknown\n282: exception is converted to a 500 status code.\n283: \n284: This conversion takes place before and after each middleware (you can think of\n285: it as the thin film in between each layer of the onion), so that every\n286: middleware can always rely on getting some kind of HTTP response back from\n287: calling its ``get_response`` callable. Middleware don't need to worry about\n288: wrapping their call to ``get_response`` in a ``try/except`` and handling an\n289: exception that might have been raised by a later middleware or the view. Even\n290: if the very next middleware in the chain raises an\n291: :class:`~django.http.Http404` exception, for example, your middleware won't see\n292: that exception; instead it will get an :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse`\n293: object with a :attr:`~django.http.HttpResponse.status_code` of 404.\n294: \n295: You can set :setting:`DEBUG_PROPAGATE_EXCEPTIONS` to ``True`` to skip this\n296: conversion and propagate exceptions upward.\n297: \n298: .. _async-middleware:\n299: \n300: Asynchronous support\n301: ====================\n302: \n303: Middleware can support any combination of synchronous and asynchronous\n304: requests. Django will adapt requests to fit the middleware's requirements if it\n305: cannot support both, but at a performance penalty.\n306: \n307: By default, Django assumes that your middleware is capable of handling only\n308: synchronous requests. To change these assumptions, set the following attributes\n309: on your middleware factory function or class:\n310: \n311: * ``sync_capable`` is a boolean indicating if the middleware can handle\n312:   synchronous requests. Defaults to ``True``.\n313: \n314: * ``async_capable`` is a boolean indicating if the middleware can handle\n315:   asynchronous requests. Defaults to ``False``.\n316: \n317: If your middleware has both ``sync_capable = True`` and\n318: ``async_capable = True``, then Django will pass it the request without\n319: converting it. In this case, you can work out if your middleware will receive\n320: async requests by checking if the ``get_response`` object you are passed is a\n321: coroutine function, using ``inspect.iscoroutinefunction``.\n322: \n323: The ``django.utils.decorators`` module contains\n324: :func:`~django.utils.decorators.sync_only_middleware`,\n325: :func:`~django.utils.decorators.async_only_middleware`, and\n326: :func:`~django.utils.decorators.sync_and_async_middleware` decorators that\n327: allow you to apply these flags to middleware factory functions.\n328: \n329: The returned callable must match the sync or async nature of the\n330: ``get_response`` method. If you have an asynchronous ``get_response``, you must\n331: return a coroutine function (``async def``).\n332: \n333: ``process_view``, ``process_template_response`` and ``process_exception``\n334: methods, if they are provided, should also be adapted to match the sync/async\n335: mode. However, Django will individually adapt them as required if you do not,\n336: at an additional performance penalty.\n337: \n338: Here's an example of how to create a middleware function that supports both::\n339: \n340:     from inspect import iscoroutinefunction\n341:     from django.utils.decorators import sync_and_async_middleware\n342: \n343: \n344:     @sync_and_async_middleware\n345:     def simple_middleware(get_response):\n346:         # One-time configuration and initialization goes here.\n347:         if iscoroutinefunction(get_response):\n348: \n349:             async def middleware(request):\n350:                 # Do something here!\n351:                 response = await get_response(request)\n352:                 return response\n353: \n354:         else:\n355: \n356:             def middleware(request):\n357:                 # Do something here!\n358:                 response = get_response(request)\n359:                 return response\n360: \n361:         return middleware\n362: \n363: .. note::\n364: \n365:     If you declare a hybrid middleware that supports both synchronous and\n366:     asynchronous calls, the kind of call you get may not match the underlying\n367:     view. Django will optimize the middleware call stack to have as few\n368:     sync/async transitions as possible.\n369: \n370:     Thus, even if you are wrapping an async view, you may be called in sync\n371:     mode if there is other, synchronous middleware between you and the view.\n372: \n373: When using an asynchronous class-based middleware, you must ensure that\n374: instances are correctly marked as coroutine functions::\n375: \n376:     from inspect import iscoroutinefunction, markcoroutinefunction\n377: \n378: \n379:     class AsyncMiddleware:\n380:         async_capable = True\n381:         sync_capable = False\n382: \n383:         def __init__(self, get_response):\n384:             self.get_response = get_response\n385:             if iscoroutinefunction(self.get_response):\n386:                 markcoroutinefunction(self)\n387: \n388:         async def __call__(self, request):\n389:             response = await self.get_response(request)\n390:             # Some logic ...\n391:             return response\n392: \n393: .. _upgrading-middleware:\n394: \n395: Upgrading pre-Django 1.10-style middleware\n396: ==========================================\n397: \n398: .. class:: django.utils.deprecation.MiddlewareMixin\n399:     :module:\n400: \n401: Django provides ``django.utils.deprecation.MiddlewareMixin`` to ease creating\n402: middleware classes that are compatible with both :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` and the\n403: old ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, and support synchronous and asynchronous requests.\n404: All middleware classes included with Django are compatible with both settings.\n405: \n406: The mixin provides an ``__init__()`` method that requires a ``get_response``\n407: argument and stores it in ``self.get_response``.\n408: \n409: The ``__call__()`` method:\n410: \n411: #. Calls ``self.process_request(request)`` (if defined).\n412: #. Calls ``self.get_response(request)`` to get the response from later\n413:    middleware and the view.\n414: #. Calls ``self.process_response(request, response)`` (if defined).\n415: #. Returns the response.\n416: \n417: If used with ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, the ``__call__()`` method will\n418: never be used; Django calls ``process_request()`` and ``process_response()``\n419: directly.\n420: \n421: In most cases, inheriting from this mixin will be sufficient to make an\n422: old-style middleware compatible with the new system with sufficient\n423: backwards-compatibility. The new short-circuiting semantics will be harmless or\n424: even beneficial to the existing middleware. In a few cases, a middleware class\n425: may need some changes to adjust to the new semantics.\n426: \n427: These are the behavioral differences between using :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` and\n428: ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``:\n429: \n430: #. Under ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, every middleware will always have its\n431:    ``process_response`` method called, even if an earlier middleware\n432:    short-circuited by returning a response from its ``process_request``\n433:    method. Under :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, middleware behaves more like an onion:\n434:    the layers that a response goes through on the way out are the same layers\n435:    that saw the request on the way in. If a middleware short-circuits, only\n436:    that middleware and the ones before it in :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` will see the\n437:    response.\n438: \n439: #. Under ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, ``process_exception`` is applied to\n440:    exceptions raised from a middleware ``process_request`` method. Under\n441:    :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, ``process_exception`` applies only to exceptions\n442:    raised from the view (or from the ``render`` method of a\n443:    :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse`). Exceptions raised from\n444:    a middleware are converted to the appropriate HTTP response and then passed\n445:    to the next middleware.\n446: \n447: #. Under ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, if a ``process_response`` method raises\n448:    an exception, the ``process_response`` methods of all earlier middleware are\n449:    skipped and a ``500 Internal Server Error`` HTTP response is always\n450:    returned (even if the exception raised was e.g. an\n451:    :class:`~django.http.Http404`). Under :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, an exception\n452:    raised from a middleware will immediately be converted to the appropriate\n453:    HTTP response, and then the next middleware in line will see that\n454:    response. Middleware are never skipped due to a middleware raising an\n455:    exception.\n456: ","url":"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/django--674eda1c@674eda1c03a3187905f48afee0f15226aa62fdf3/-/blob/docs/topics/http/middleware.txt"}}}

`django/core/handlers/wsgi.py` - Contains the WSGI handler that initializes middleware\n   - `django/core/handlers/asgi.py` - Contains the ASGI handler for async middleware support\n   - where each middleware wraps the next one\n\n4. **Process request/response calling order** (the \"onion\" pattern):\n   - Request phase: Top-down through the middleware list as defined in MIDDLEWARE\n   - Response phase: Bottom-up (reverse order) through the middleware that saw the request\n   - Early returns from middleware prevent inner layers from processing the request\n\n5. **Comparison with WSGI middleware**:\n   - Django's approach differs fundamentally from WSGI, using a callable chain pattern instead of iterator-based middleware\n   - Django provides specialized hook methods like Processing\\n\\nDjango's middleware system is implemented through a sophisticated callable chain architecture that wraps request/response handling. The implementation spans multiple core files:\\n\\n### Middleware Stack Construction\\n\\nThe primary entry point for middleware processing is in `django/core/handlers/base.py`, where the `BaseHandler` class implements the `load_middleware()` method (lines 27-104). This method is called during handler initialization to populate the middleware stack from the `settings.MIDDLEWARE` configuration.\\n\\n**Key construction details:**\\n\\n1. **Reversed iteration** (line 41): The middleware list is iterated in reverse order (`for middleware_path in reversed(settings.MIDDLEWARE)`). This creates a nested chain where each middleware wraps the next one, effectively building an \\\"onion\\\" structure.\\n\\n2. **Dynamic import** (line 42): Each middleware class/factory is imported using `import_string(middleware_path)` from `django.utils.module_loading`.\\n\\n3. **Chaining mechanism** (line 63): Each middleware instance is created by calling the middleware factory with an `adapted_handler` parameter. This handler is either the view wrapper or the next middleware in the chain, creating a functional composition pattern.\\n\\n4. **Async/sync adaptation** (lines 50-61): Django detects whether middleware supports synchronous and/or asynchronous requests via `sync_capable` and `async_capable` attributes and adapts the handler accordingly.\\n\\n5. **Exception wrapping** (lines 39, 97): Each middleware is wrapped with `convert_exception_to_response()` to ensure exceptions are converted to HTTP responses before passing to the next middleware.\\n\\n### Request/Response Processing Order\\n\\nThe middleware processing order follows the \\\"onion\\\" pattern documented in `docs/topics/http/middleware.txt` (lines 135-151):\\n\\n**Request phase (top-down):**\\nRequests pass through middleware in the order defined in `settings.MIDDLEWARE`, from top to bottom. This is enforced through the nested callable structure created during initialization.\\n\\n**Response phase (bottom-up):**\\nResponses return through middleware in reverse order - only through the middleware layers that the request actually passed through. This is implemented via the nested callable chain: when middleware calls `get_response()` and receives a response, it processes that response before returning it.\\n\\n### Middleware Hook Methods\\n\\nThe `MiddlewareMixin` class in `django/utils/deprecation.py` (lines 275-331) provides the standard interface for middleware:\\n\\n1. **`__init__(get_response)`** (lines 279-290): Called once when the server starts, receives the next callable in the chain.\\n\\n2. **`__call__(request)`** (lines 302-312): Invokes in this order for synchronous requests:\\n   - Calls `process_request()` if defined\\n   - Calls `get_response(request)` to invoke the next middleware/view\\n   - Calls `process_response()` if defined\\n   - Returns the response\\n\\n3. **`__acall__(request)`** (lines 314-331): Async version with identical semantics but using `await` and `sync_to_async` adaptation.\\n\\n### View, Exception, and Template Response Middleware\\n\\nThe `_get_response()` and `_get_response_async()` methods (lines 176-228 and 230-300 in base.py) handle special middleware hooks:\\n\\n- **View middleware** (line 79-83): Collected in reverse order, executed in forward order just before the view\\n- **Template response middleware** (lines 84-89, 211-220): Applied after the view returns if the response has a render method\\n- **Exception middleware** (lines 90-95, 363-366): Called when exceptions occur, executed in reverse order\\n\\n### Exception Handling\\n\\nThe `convert_exception_to_response()` function in `django/core/handlers/exception.py` (lines 25-61) is a critical component. It wraps each middleware with exception handling that converts exceptions to HTTP responses, preventing middleware from leaking exceptions and ensuring each middleware can rely on receiving responses rather than exceptions.\\n\\n### Comparison with WSGI Middleware\\n\\nDjango's MIDDLEWARE differs fundamentally from traditional WSGI middleware:\\n\\n1. **WSGI pattern**: Traditional WSGI middleware wraps an application returning an iterator, using the nested callable pattern but operating at the HTTP request/response level with different semantics.\\n\\n2. **Django pattern**: Django middleware uses a factory function/class taking a callable (`get_response`) and returning a middleware callable. The middleware is responsible for calling `get_response()` to proceed, creating a more explicit control flow.\\n\\n3. **Short-circuiting behavior** (lines 148-151 in middleware.txt): Django's middleware can return a response without calling `get_response()`, which prevents inner middleware from seeing the request/response. This is different from WSGI where all middleware typically see all requests.\\n\\n4. **Exception handling**: Django's middleware have explicit exception handling via `process_exception()` hooks that run in reverse order, whereas WSGI relies on exception propagation.\\n\\n5. **Special hooks**: Django provides `process_view()`, `process_template_response()`, and `process_exception()` as special hooks beyond the basic request/response cycle, enabling more granular control over request processing phases.\\n\\n### Handler Integration\\n\\nThe concrete implementations in `django/core/handlers/wsgi.py` (lines 113-144) and `django/core/handlers/asgi.py` (lines 143-152) show how the middleware system is initialized and used:\\n\\n- **WSGIHandler.__init__** calls `self.load_middleware()` during instantiation\\n- **ASGIHandler.__init__** calls `self.load_middleware(is_async=True)` for async context\\n- Both handlers call `get_response()` (lines 124 in wsgi.py, 224 in asgi.py) which invokes the constructed `_middleware_chain`\\n\\nThis design ensures Django's middleware system provides a robust, layered architecture for request/response processing with proper exception handling, async support, and explicit control flow through the callable chain.\"\n}\n"},"caller":{"type":"direct"}}],"stop_reason":null,"stop_sequence":null,"usage":{"input_tokens":9,"cache_creation_input_tokens":435,"cache_read_input_tokens":74183,"cache_creation":{"ephemeral_5m_input_tokens":0,"ephemeral_1h_input_tokens":435},"output_tokens":5,"service_tier":"standard","inference_geo":"not_available"},"context_management":null},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"81a8b0db-1e09-4b2a-99ee-598a1e3c7cf3","uuid":"08360614-756b-42d6-aa2e-0b07fb0569e8"}
